<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 13</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Aluminium,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">13</span></sub>Al</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium-4.jpg"><img alt="Aluminium-4.jpg" data-file-height="706" data-file-width="706" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Aluminium-4.jpg/220px-Aluminium-4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Aluminium-4.jpg/330px-Aluminium-4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Aluminium-4.jpg/440px-Aluminium-4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#cccccc">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<div class="plainlist" style="margin: 0; text-align: left;"><ul><li style="margin: 0; text-indent: -1em; padding-left: 1em;"><i>aluminium</i>: <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="/lj/: 'l' in 'lute'">lj</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-aluminium.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/En-uk-aluminium.ogg" title="En-uk-aluminium.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">AL</span>-yuu-<span style="font-size:90%">MIN</span>-ee-əm</i></a>)</li><li style="margin: 0; text-indent: -1em; padding-left: 1em;"><i>aluminum</i>: <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/lj/: 'l' in 'lute'">lj</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-aluminum.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/En-us-aluminum.ogg" title="En-us-aluminum.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span><br/>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ə-<span style="font-size:90%">LEW</span>-min-əm</i></a>)</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Alternative name</th><td>
aluminum (U.S., Canada)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
silvery gray metallic</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><span style="font-weight:normal;"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_aluminium" title="Template:Infobox aluminium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_aluminium" title="Template talk:Infobox aluminium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_aluminium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Aluminium</b> or <b>aluminum</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>Al</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 13. It is a silvery-white, soft, nonmagnetic and <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a> <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Boron_group" title="Boron group">boron group</a>. By mass, aluminium makes up about 8% of the  <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>; it is the third most abundant element after <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and the most abundant metal in the crust, though it is less common in the mantle below. The chief <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> of aluminium is <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a>. Aluminium metal is so chemically reactive that <a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native specimens</a> are rare and limited to extreme <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reducing</a> environments. Instead, it is found combined in over 270 different <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is remarkable for its low <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and its ability to resist <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> through the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>. Aluminium and its <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">alloys</a> are vital to the <a href="/wiki/Aerospace" title="Aerospace">aerospace</a> industry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[6]</a></sup> and important in <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transportation</a> and building industries, such as building facades and window frames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxides" title="Oxides">oxides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfates" title="Sulfates">sulfates</a> are the most useful compounds of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite its prevalence in the environment, no known form of life uses aluminium <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolically" title="Metabolically">metabolically</a>, but aluminium is well tolerated by plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-8">[8]</a></sup> Because of these salts' abundance, the potential for a biological role for them is of continuing interest, and studies continue.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclei_and_isotopes">Nuclei and isotopes</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_aluminium" title="Isotopes of aluminium">Isotopes of aluminium</a></div>
<p>Aluminium's <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> is 13. Of aluminium isotopes, only one is stable: <sup>27</sup>Al. This is consistent with the fact aluminium's atomic number is odd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[a]</a></sup> It is the only <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">isotope that has existed on Earth in its current form since the creation of the planet</a>. It is essentially the only isotope representing the element on Earth, which makes aluminium a <a href="/wiki/Mononuclidic_element" title="Mononuclidic element">mononuclidic element</a> and practically equates its <a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">standard atomic weight</a> to that of the isotope. Such a low standard atomic weight of aluminium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[b]</a></sup> has some effects on the properties of the element (see <a href="#Bulk">below</a>).
</p><p>All other isotopes are <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> and could not have survived; the most stable isotope of these is <sup>26</sup>Al (<a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> 720,000 years). <sup>26</sup>Al is produced from <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">argon</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a> by <a href="/wiki/Spallation" title="Spallation">spallation</a> caused by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> protons and used in <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiodating</a>. The ratio of <sup>26</sup>Al to <sup>10</sup><a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">Be</a> has been used to study transport, deposition, <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">sediment</a> storage, burial times, and erosion on 10<sup>5</sup> to 10<sup>6</sup> year time scales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> Most meteorite scientists believe that the energy released by the decay of <sup>26</sup>Al was responsible for the melting and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">differentiation</a> of some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a> after their formation 4.55 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The remaining isotopes of aluminium, with mass numbers ranging from 21 to 43, all have half-lives well under an hour. Three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metastable" title="Metastable">metastable</a> states are known, all with half-lives under a minute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron_shell">Electron shell</span></h3>
<p>An aluminium atom has 13 electrons, arranged in an <a href="/wiki/Electron_configuration" title="Electron configuration">electron configuration</a> of [<a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">Ne</a>]3s<sup>2</sup>3p<sup>1</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.2_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.2-14">[12]</a></sup> with three electrons beyond a stable noble gas configuration. Accordingly, the combined first three <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energies</a> of aluminium are far lower than the fourth ionization energy alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.6_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.6-15">[13]</a></sup> Aluminium can relatively easily surrender its three outermost electrons in many chemical reactions (see <a href="#Chemistry">below</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a> of aluminium is 1.61 (Pauling scale).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.29_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.29-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>A free aluminium atom has a <a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">radius</a> of 143 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picometer" title="Picometer">pm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30-17">[15]</a></sup> With the three outermost electrons removed, the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_radius" title="Ionic radius">radius</a> shrinks to 39 pm for a 4-coordinated atom or 53.5 pm for a 6-coordinated atom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDean19994.30-17">[15]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, aluminium atoms (when not affected by atoms of other elements) form a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cubic crystal system</a> bound by <a href="/wiki/Metallic_bonding" title="Metallic bonding">metallic bonding</a> provided by atoms' outermost electrons; hence aluminium (at these conditions) is a metal. This crystal system is shared by some other metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>; the size of a unit cell of aluminium is comparable to that of those other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enghag2008_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enghag2008-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bulk">Bulk</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg/220px-Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg/330px-Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg/440px-Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium_bar_surface_etched.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Etched surface from a high purity (99.9998%) aluminium bar, size 55×37 mm</div></div></div>
<p>Aluminium metal, when in quantity, is very shiny and resembles <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> because it preferentially absorbs <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">far ultraviolet</a> radiation while reflecting all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a> so it does not impart any color to reflected light, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Reflectance" title="Reflectance">reflectance spectra</a> of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>. Another important characteristic of aluminium is its low density, 2.70 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELide20044-3_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELide20044-3-19">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[c]</a></sup> Aluminium is a relatively soft, durable, lightweight, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleable" title="Malleable">malleable</a> with appearance ranging from silvery to dull gray, depending on the surface roughness. It is nonmagnetic and does not easily ignite. A fresh film of aluminium serves as a good reflector (approximately 92%) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a> and an excellent reflector (as much as 98%) of medium and far <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation. The <a href="/wiki/Yield_(engineering)" title="Yield (engineering)">yield strength</a> of pure aluminium is 7–11 <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">MPa</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">aluminium alloys</a> have yield strengths ranging from 200 MPa to 600 MPa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polmear1995_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polmear1995-21">[18]</a></sup> Aluminium has about one-third the <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elastic_modulus" title="Elastic modulus">stiffness</a> of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>. It is easily <a href="/wiki/Machining" title="Machining">machined</a>, <a href="/wiki/Casting_(metalworking)" title="Casting (metalworking)">cast</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drawing_(metalworking)" title="Drawing (metalworking)">drawn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Extrusion" title="Extrusion">extruded</a>.
</p><p>Aluminium atoms are arranged in a <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">face-centered cubic</a> (fcc) structure. Aluminium has a <a href="/wiki/Stacking-fault_energy" title="Stacking-fault energy">stacking-fault energy</a> of approximately 200 mJ/m<sup>2</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is a good <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_conduction" title="Heat conduction">thermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductor</a>, having 59% the conductivity of copper, both thermal and electrical, while having only 30% of <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper's</a> density. Aluminium is capable of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superconductor" title="Superconductor">superconductivity</a>, with a superconducting critical temperature of 1.2 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> and a critical magnetic field of about 100 <a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (10 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millitesla" title="Millitesla">milliteslas</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup>
Aluminium is the most common material for the fabrication of superconducting <a href="/wiki/Qubit" title="Qubit">qubits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">Corrosion</a> resistance can be excellent because a thin surface layer of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> forms when the bare metal is exposed to air, effectively preventing further <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CorrAl_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-CorrAl-25">[22]</a></sup> in a process termed <a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>.  The strongest aluminium alloys are less corrosion resistant due to <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_cell" title="Galvanic cell">galvanic</a> reactions with alloyed <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polmear1995_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Polmear1995-21">[18]</a></sup> This corrosion resistance is greatly reduced by aqueous salts, particularly in the presence of dissimilar metals.
</p><p>In highly acidic solutions, aluminium reacts with water to form hydrogen, and in highly alkaline ones to form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminates" title="Aluminates">aluminates</a>— protective passivation under these conditions is negligible.  Primarily because it is corroded by dissolved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorides" title="Chlorides">chlorides</a>, such as common <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a>, household plumbing is never made from aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beal1999_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beal1999-26">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, because of its general resistance to corrosion, aluminium is one of the few metals that retains silvery reflectance in finely powdered form, making it an important component of <a href="/wiki/Silver_(color)" title="Silver (color)">silver-colored</a> paints. Aluminium mirror finish has the highest <a href="/wiki/Reflectance" title="Reflectance">reflectance</a> of any metal in the 200–400 nm (<a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a>) and the 3,000–10,000 nm (far <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a>) regions; in the 400–700 nm visible range it is slightly outperformed by <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> and in the 700–3000 nm (near IR) by <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidized" title="Oxidized">oxidized</a> by <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> at temperatures below 280 °C to produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">aluminium hydroxide</a> and heat:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Al + 6 H<sub>2</sub>O → 2 Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>This conversion is of interest for the production of hydrogen. However, commercial application of this fact has challenges in circumventing the passivating oxide layer, which inhibits the reaction, and in storing the energy required to regenerate the aluminium metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_compounds">Inorganic compounds</span></h3>
<p>The vast majority of compounds, including all Al-containing minerals and all commercially significant aluminium compounds, feature aluminium in the oxidation state 3+. The <a href="/wiki/Coordination_number" title="Coordination number">coordination number</a> of such compounds varies, but generally Al<sup>3+</sup> is six-coordinate or tetracoordinate. Almost all compounds of aluminium(III) are colorless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>All four <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">trihalides</a> are well known. Unlike the structures of the three heavier trihalides, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_fluoride" title="Aluminium fluoride">aluminium fluoride</a> (AlF<sub>3</sub>) features six-coordinate Al. The octahedral coordination environment for AlF<sub>3</sub> is related to the compactness of the <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> ion, six of which can fit around the small Al<sup>3+</sup> center. AlF<sub>3</sub> sublimes (with cracking) at 1,291 °C (2,356 °F). With heavier halides, the coordination numbers are lower. The other trihalides are <a href="/wiki/Dimer_(chemistry)" title="Dimer (chemistry)">dimeric</a> or <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymeric</a> with tetrahedral Al centers. These materials are prepared by treating aluminium metal with the halogen, although other methods exist. <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">Acidification</a> of the oxides or <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a> affords hydrates. In aqueous solution, the halides often form mixtures, generally containing six-coordinate Al centers that feature both halide and <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">aquo ligands</a>. When aluminium and fluoride are together in aqueous solution, they readily form complex ions such as <span class="chemf nowrap">[AlF(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">AlF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">[AlF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. In the case of chloride, polyaluminium clusters are formed such as [Al<sub>13</sub>O<sub>4</sub>(OH)<sub>24</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>12</sub>]<sup>7+</sup>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlHydrolysis.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="278" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/AlHydrolysis.png/220px-AlHydrolysis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/83/AlHydrolysis.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlHydrolysis.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aluminium hydrolysis as a function of pH.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Coordinated water molecules are omitted.</div></div></div>
<p>Aluminium forms one stable oxide with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3.</sub> It can be found in nature in the mineral <a href="/wiki/Corundum" title="Corundum">corundum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> Aluminium oxide is also commonly called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">Sapphire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">ruby</a> are impure corundum contaminated with trace amounts of other metals. The two oxide-hydroxides, AlO(OH), are <a href="/wiki/Boehmite" title="Boehmite">boehmite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diaspore" title="Diaspore">diaspore</a>. There are three trihydroxides: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayerite" title="Bayerite">bayerite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gibbsite" title="Gibbsite">gibbsite</a>, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nordstrandite&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nordstrandite (page does not exist)">nordstrandite</a>, which differ in their crystalline structure (<a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">polymorphs</a>). Most are produced from ores by a variety of wet processes using acid and base. Heating the hydroxides leads to formation of corundum. These materials are of central importance to the production of aluminium and are themselves extremely useful.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_carbide" title="Aluminium carbide">Aluminium carbide</a> (Al<sub>4</sub>C<sub>3</sub>) is made by heating a mixture of the elements above 1,000 °C (1,832 °F). The pale yellow crystals consist of tetrahedral aluminium centers. It reacts with water or dilute acids to give <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_acetylide" title="Metal acetylide">acetylide</a>, Al<sub>2</sub>(C<sub>2</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, is made by passing <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> over heated aluminium.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_nitride" title="Aluminium nitride">Aluminium nitride</a> (AlN) is the only nitride known for aluminium. Unlike the oxides, it features tetrahedral Al centers. It can be made from the elements at 800 °C (1,472 °F). It is air-stable material with a usefully high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_phosphide" title="Aluminium phosphide">Aluminium phosphide</a> (AlP) is made similarly; it hydrolyses to give <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>AlP + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O → Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> + PH<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rarer_oxidation_states">Rarer oxidation states</span></h4>
<p>Although the great majority of aluminium compounds feature Al<sup>3+</sup> centers, compounds with lower oxidation states are known and sometime of significance as precursors to the Al<sup>3+</sup> species.
</p>
<h5><span id="Aluminium.28I.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Aluminium(I)">Aluminium(I)</span></h5>
<p>AlF, AlCl and AlBr exist in the gaseous phase when the trihalide is heated with aluminium. The composition <span class="texhtml">AlI</span> is unstable at room temperature, converting to triiodide:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-al1_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-al1-32">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {3 AlI -&gt; AlI3 + 2 Al}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>3</mn>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>

</mrow>

<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\ce {3 AlI -&gt; AlI3 + 2 Al}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {3 AlI -&gt; AlI3 + 2 Al}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7a1434df9b7440abbf2c9d11bf2b747320820a80" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:20.939ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>A stable derivative of aluminium monoiodide is the cyclic <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adduct</a> formed with <a href="/wiki/Triethylamine" title="Triethylamine">triethylamine</a>, <span class="texhtml">Al<sub>4</sub>I<sub>4</sub>(NEt<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub></span>. Also of theoretical interest but only of fleeting existence are Al<sub>2</sub>O and Al<sub>2</sub>S. Al<sub>2</sub>O is made by heating the normal oxide, Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, with silicon at 1,800 °C (3,272 °F) in a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-al1_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-al1-32">[29]</a></sup> Such materials quickly <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionate</a> to the starting materials.
</p>
<h5><span id="Aluminium.28II.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Aluminium(II)">Aluminium(II)</span></h5>
<p>Very simple Al(II) compounds are invoked or observed in the reactions of Al metal with oxidants. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_monoxide" title="Aluminium monoxide">aluminium monoxide</a>, AlO, has been detected in the gas phase after explosion<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup> and in stellar absorption spectra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup> More thoroughly investigated are compounds of the formula R<sub>4</sub>Al<sub>2</sub> which contain an Al-Al bond and where R is a large organic <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organoaluminium_compounds_and_related_hydrides">Organoaluminium compounds and related hydrides</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organoaluminium_compound" title="Organoaluminium compound">Organoaluminium compound</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="786" data-file-width="1100" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png/220px-Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png/330px-Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png/440px-Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trimethylaluminium-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of <a href="/wiki/Trimethylaluminium" title="Trimethylaluminium">trimethylaluminium</a>, a compound that features five-coordinate carbon.</div></div></div>
<p>A variety of compounds of empirical formula AlR<sub>3</sub> and AlR<sub>1.5</sub>Cl<sub>1.5</sub> exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> These species usually feature tetrahedral Al centers formed by dimerization with some R or Cl bridging between both Al atoms, e.g. "<a href="/wiki/Trimethylaluminium" title="Trimethylaluminium">trimethylaluminium</a>" has the formula Al<sub>2</sub>(CH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>6</sub> (see figure). With large organic groups, triorganoaluminium compounds exist as three-coordinate monomers, such as <a href="/wiki/Triisobutylaluminium" title="Triisobutylaluminium">triisobutylaluminium</a>. Such compounds<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2016)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> are widely used in industrial chemistry, despite the fact that they are often highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrophoric" title="Pyrophoric">pyrophoric</a>. Few analogues exist between <a href="/wiki/Organoaluminium_chemistry" title="Organoaluminium chemistry">organoaluminium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organoboron_chemistry" title="Organoboron chemistry">organoboron</a> compounds other than<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> large organic groups.
</p><p>The industrially important aluminium hydride is <a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">lithium aluminium hydride</a> (LiAlH<sub>4</sub>), which is used in as a reducing agent in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. It can be produced from <a href="/wiki/Lithium_hydride" title="Lithium hydride">lithium hydride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium trichloride</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>4 LiH + AlCl<sub>3</sub> → LiAlH<sub>4</sub> + 3 LiCl</dd></dl>
<p>Several useful derivatives of LiAlH<sub>4</sub> are known, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red-Al" title="Red-Al">sodium bis(2-methoxyethoxy)dihydridoaluminate</a>. The simplest hydride, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydride" title="Aluminium hydride">aluminium hydride</a> or alane, remains a laboratory curiosity. It is a polymer with the formula (AlH<sub>3</sub>)<sub><i>n</i></sub>, in contrast to the corresponding boron hydride that is a dimer with the formula (BH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_bauxite_production" title="List of countries by bauxite production">List of countries by bauxite production</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_space">In space</span></h3>
<p>Aluminium's per-particle abundance in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is 3.15 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> (parts per million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodders_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[d]</a></sup> It is the twelfth most abundant of all elements and third most abundant among the elements that have odd atomic numbers, after hydrogen and nitrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lodders_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lodders-37">[34]</a></sup> The only stable isotope of aluminium, <sup>27</sup>Al, is the eighteenth most abundant nucleus in the Universe. It is created almost entirely after fusion of carbon in massive stars that will later become <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type II supernovae</a>: this fusion creates <sup>26</sup>Mg, which, upon capturing free protons and neutrons becomes aluminium. Some smaller quantities of <sup>27</sup>Al are created in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_burning" title="Hydrogen burning">hydrogen burning</a> shells of evolved stars, where <sup>26</sup>Mg can capture free protons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton2007_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2007-39">[35]</a></sup> Essentially all aluminium now in existence is <sup>27</sup>Al; <sup>26</sup>Al was present in the early Solar System but is currently <a href="/wiki/Extinct_radionuclide" title="Extinct radionuclide">extinct</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Trace_radioisotope" title="Trace radioisotope">trace quantities</a> of <sup>26</sup>Al that do exist are the most common <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma ray</a> emitter in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_gas" title="Interstellar gas">interstellar gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton2007_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2007-39">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Earth">On Earth</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG/220px-Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG/330px-Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG/440px-Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bauxite_h%C3%A9rault.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bauxite, a major aluminium ore. The red-brown color is due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> minerals.</div></div></div>
<p>Overall, the Earth is about 1.59% aluminium by mass (seventh in abundance by mass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mit1_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-mit1-40">[36]</a></sup> Aluminium occurs in greater proportion in the Earth than in the Universe because aluminium easily forms the oxide and becomes bound into rocks and aluminium stays in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a> while less reactive metals sink to the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clayton2007_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clayton2007-39">[35]</a></sup> In the Earth's crust, aluminium is the most abundant (8.3% by mass) metallic element and the third most abundant of all elements (after oxygen and silicon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood1997_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood1997-29">[26]</a></sup> A large number of silicates in the Earth's crust contain aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WadeBanister2016_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-WadeBanister2016-41">[37]</a></sup> In contrast, the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> is only 2.38% aluminium by mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of its strong affinity for oxygen, aluminium is almost never found in the elemental state; instead it is found in oxides or silicates. <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">Feldspars</a>, the most common group of minerals in the Earth's crust, are aluminosilicates. Aluminium also occurs in the minerals <a href="/wiki/Beryl" title="Beryl">beryl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spinel" title="Spinel">spinel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turquoise" title="Turquoise">turquoise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup> Impurities in Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, such as <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, yield the <a href="/wiki/Gemstone" title="Gemstone">gemstones</a> <a href="/wiki/Ruby" title="Ruby">ruby</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sapphire" title="Sapphire">sapphire</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KotzTreichel2012_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-KotzTreichel2012-44">[40]</a></sup> Native aluminium metal can only be found as a minor phase in low oxygen <a href="/wiki/Fugacity" title="Fugacity">fugacity</a> environments, such as the interiors of certain volcanoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> Native aluminium has been reported in <a href="/wiki/Cold_seep" title="Cold seep">cold seeps</a> in the northeastern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a> of the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>. It is possible that these deposits resulted from <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> of tetrahydroxoaluminate Al(OH)<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_2011_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chen_2011-46">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although aluminium is a common and widespread element, not all aluminium minerals are economically viable sources of the metal. Almost all metallic aluminium is produced from the <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a> (AlO<sub><i>x</i></sub>(OH)<sub>3–2<i>x</i></sub>). Bauxite occurs as a <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> product of low iron and silica bedrock in tropical climatic conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup> In 2017, most bauxite was mined in Australia, China, Guinea, and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_aluminium" title="History of aluminium">History of aluminium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="898" data-file-width="800" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg/170px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg/255px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg/340px-Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler_Litho.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a>, the chemist who first thoroughly described metallic elemental aluminium</div></div></div>
<p>The history of aluminium has been shaped by usage of <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>. The first written record of alum, made by <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>, dates back to the 5th century BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712-49">[45]</a></sup> The ancients are known to have used alum as a dyeing mordant and for city defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200712-49">[45]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>, alum, an indispensable good in the European fabric industry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ClaphamPower1941_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ClaphamPower1941-50">[46]</a></sup> was a subject of international commerce;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200716_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200716-51">[47]</a></sup> it was imported to Europe from the eastern Mediterranean until the mid-15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nature of alum remained unknown. Around 1530, Swiss physician <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a> suggested alum was a salt of an earth of alum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200725_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200725-53">[49]</a></sup> In 1595, German doctor and chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Libavius" title="Andreas Libavius">Andreas Libavius</a> experimentally confirmed this;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks1968_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks1968-54">[50]</a></sup> In 1722, German chemist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hoffmann" title="Friedrich Hoffmann">Friedrich Hoffmann</a> announced his belief that the base of alum was a distinct earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18962_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18962-55">[51]</a></sup> In 1754, German chemist <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Sigismund_Marggraf" title="Andreas Sigismund Marggraf">Andreas Sigismund Marggraf</a> synthesized alumina by boiling clay in sulfuric acid and subsequently adding <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18962_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18962-55">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Attempts to produce aluminium metal date back to 1760.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18963_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18963-56">[52]</a></sup> The first successful attempt, however, was completed in 1824 by Danish physicist and chemist <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_%C3%98rsted" title="Hans Christian Ørsted">Hans Christian Ørsted</a>. He reacted <a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">anhydrous</a> <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> <a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry)" title="Amalgam (chemistry)">amalgam</a>, yielding a lump of metal looking similar to tin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-(København)1827_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-(København)1827-57">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woehler_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-woehler-58">[54]</a></sup> He presented his results and demonstrated a sample of the new metal in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200736_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200736-59">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FontaniCosta2014_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FontaniCosta2014-60">[56]</a></sup> In 1827, German chemist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> repeated Ørsted's experiments but did not identify any aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetski_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Venetski-61">[57]</a></sup> (The reason for this inconsistency was only discovered in 1921.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738-62">[58]</a></sup> He conducted a similar experiment in 1827 by mixing anhydrous aluminium chloride with potassium and produced a powder of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-woehler_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-woehler-58">[54]</a></sup> In 1845, he was able to produce small pieces of the metal and described some physical properties of this metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200738-62">[58]</a></sup> For many years thereafter, Wöhler was credited as the discoverer of aluminium following the success and descriptive details of his 1845 experiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woehler_Oersted_Fogh_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woehler_Oersted_Fogh-63">[59]</a></sup> As Wöhler's method could not yield great quantities of aluminium, the metal remained rare; its cost exceeded that of gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Venetski_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Venetski-61">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="741" data-file-width="500" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg/170px-Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg/255px-Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg/340px-Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eros-piccadilly-circus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The statue of <a href="/wiki/Anteros" title="Anteros">Anteros</a> in <a href="/wiki/Piccadilly_Circus" title="Piccadilly Circus">Piccadilly Circus</a>, London, was made in 1893 and is one of the first statues cast in aluminium.</div></div></div>
<p>French chemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henri_Etienne_Sainte-Claire_Deville" title="Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville">Henri Etienne Sainte-Claire Deville</a> announced an industrial method of aluminium production in 1854 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Paris Academy of Sciences">Paris Academy of Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200739_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200739-64">[60]</a></sup> Aluminium trichloride could be reduced by sodium, which was more convenient and less expensive than potassium, which Wöhler had used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> In 1856, Deville along with companions established the world's first industrial production of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200739_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200739-64">[60]</a></sup> From 1855 to 1859, the price of aluminium dropped by an order of magnitude, from US$500 to $40 per pound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polmear2005_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polmear2005-66">[62]</a></sup> Even then, aluminium was still not of great purity and produced aluminium differed in properties by sample.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200746_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200746-67">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first industrial large-scale production method was independently developed in 1886 by French engineer <a href="/wiki/Paul_H%C3%A9roult" title="Paul Héroult">Paul Héroult</a> and American engineer <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martin_Hall" title="Charles Martin Hall">Charles Martin Hall</a>; it is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200755–61_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200755–61-68">[64]</a></sup> The Hall–Héroult process converts alumina into the metal. Austrian chemist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Josef_Bayer" title="Carl Josef Bayer">Carl Joseph Bayer</a> discovered a way of purifying bauxite to yield alumina, now known as the <a href="/wiki/Bayer_process" title="Bayer process">Bayer process</a>, in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200774_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200774-69">[65]</a></sup> Modern production of the aluminium metal is based on the Bayer and Hall–Héroult processes. The Hall–Héroult process was further improved in 1920 by a team led by Swedish chemist Carl Wilhelm Söderberg; this improvement greatly increased the world output of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aluminiumleader_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-aluminiumleader-70">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prices of aluminium dropped and aluminium became widely used in jewelry, everyday items, eyeglass frames, optical instruments, tableware, and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_foil" title="Aluminium foil">foil</a> in the 1890s and early 20th century. Aluminium's ability to form hard yet light alloys with other metals provided the metal many uses at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200764–69_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200764–69-71">[67]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, major governments demanded large shipments of aluminium for light strong airframes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-20th century, aluminium had become a part of everyday life and an essential component of housewares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200769–70_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200769–70-73">[69]</a></sup> During the mid-20th century, aluminium emerged as a civil engineering material, with buildings using for both basic construction and interior,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007165–166_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007165–166-74">[70]</a></sup> and advanced its use in military engineering, for both airplanes and land armor vehicle engines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200785_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov200785-75">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Earth's first artificial satellite</a>, launched in 1957, consisted of two separate aluminium semi-spheres joined together and all subsequent space vehicles have been made of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aluminiumleader_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-aluminiumleader-70">[66]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_can" title="Aluminium can">aluminium can</a> was invented in 1956 and employed as a storage for drinks in 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007135_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrozdov2007135-76">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/w/index.php?title=File:Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg&amp;lang=en"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="639" data-file-width="750" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg/220px-Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg/330px-Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg/440px-Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aluminium_-_world_production_trend.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production of aluminium since 1900</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout the 20th century, the production of aluminium rose rapidly: while the world production of aluminium in 1900 was 6,800 metric tons, the annual production first exceeded 100,000 metric tons in 1916; 1,000,000 tons in 1941; 10,000,000 tons in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-77">[73]</a></sup> In the 1970s, the increased demand for aluminium made it an exchange commodity; it entered the <a href="/wiki/London_Metal_Exchange" title="London Metal Exchange">London Metal Exchange</a>, the oldest industrial metal exchange in the world, in 1978.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aluminiumleader_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-aluminiumleader-70">[66]</a></sup> The output continued to grow: the annual production of aluminium exceeded 50,000,000 metric tons in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-77">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_price" title="Real price">real price</a> for aluminium declined from $14,000 per metric ton in 1900 to $2,340 in 1948 (in 1998 United States dollars).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-77">[73]</a></sup> Extraction and processing costs were lowered over technological progress and the scale of the economies. However, the need to exploit lower-grade poorer quality deposits and the use of fast increasing input costs (above all, energy) increased the net cost of aluminium;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi20139_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi20139-78">[74]</a></sup> the real price began to grow in the 1970s with the rise of energy cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi20139–10_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi20139–10-79">[75]</a></sup> Production moved from the industrialized countries to countries where production was cheaper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201310_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201310-80">[76]</a></sup> Production costs in the late 20th century changed because of advances in technology, lower energy prices, exchange rates of the United States dollar, and alumina prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201314–15_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201314–15-81">[77]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/BRIC" title="BRIC">BRIC</a> countries' combined share grew in the first decade of the 21st century from 32.6% to 56.5% in primary production and 21.4% to 47.8% in primary consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201317_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201317-82">[78]</a></sup> China is accumulating an especially large share of world's production thanks to abundance of resources, cheap energy, and governmental stimuli;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201320_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201320-83">[79]</a></sup> it also increased its consumption share from 2% in 1972 to 40% in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201322_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201322-84">[80]</a></sup> In the United States, Western Europe, and Japan, most aluminium was consumed in transportation, engineering, construction, and packaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENappi201323_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENappi201323-85">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>Aluminium is named after alumina, or aluminium oxide in modern nomenclature. The word "alumina" comes from "alum", the mineral from which it was collected. The word "alum" comes from <i>alumen</i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> word meaning "bitter salt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> The word <i>alumen</i> stems from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> root <i>*alu-</i> meaning "bitter" or "beer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2370" data-file-width="1500" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg/220px-The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg/330px-The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg/440px-The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Turner_Brass_Works_ad_1897.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1897 American advertisement featuring the <i>aluminum</i> spelling</div></div></div>
<p>British chemist <a href="/wiki/Humphry_Davy" title="Humphry Davy">Humphry Davy</a>, who performed a number of experiments aimed to synthesize the metal, is credited as the person who named the element. In 1808, he suggested the metal be named <i>alumium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy1808_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1808-88">[84]</a></sup> This suggestion was criticized by contemporary chemists from France, Germany, and Sweden, who insisted the metal should be named for the oxide, alumina, from which it would be isolated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18963–4_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18963–4-89">[85]</a></sup> In 1812, Davy chose <i>aluminum</i>, thus producing the modern name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davy1812_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davy1812-90">[86]</a></sup> However, it is spelled and pronounced differently outside of North America: <i>aluminum</i> is in use in the U.S. and Canada while <i>aluminium</i> is in use elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell2015_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Powell2015-91">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling">Spelling</span></h3>
<p>The <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> suffix followed the precedent set in other newly discovered elements of the time: potassium, sodium, magnesium, calcium, and <a href="/wiki/Strontium" title="Strontium">strontium</a> (all of which Davy isolated himself). Nevertheless, element names ending in <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> were known at the time; for example, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> (known to Europeans since the 16th century), <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> (discovered in 1778), and <a href="/wiki/Tantalum" title="Tantalum">tantalum</a> (discovered in 1802). The <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> suffix is consistent with the universal spelling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alumina" title="Alumina">alumina</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> (as opposed to aluminia); compare to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lanthana" title="Lanthana">lanthana</a>, the oxide of <a href="/wiki/Lanthanum" title="Lanthanum">lanthanum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_oxide" title="Magnesium oxide">magnesia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceria" title="Ceria">ceria</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thoria" title="Thoria">thoria</a>, the oxides of <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerium" title="Cerium">cerium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, respectively.
</p><p>In 1812, British scientist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Young_(scientist)" title="Thomas Young (scientist)">Thomas Young</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup> wrote an anonymous review of Davy's book, in which he objected to <i>aluminum</i> and proposed the name <i>aluminium</i>: "for so we shall take the liberty of writing the word, in preference to aluminum, which has a less classical sound."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup> This name did catch on: while the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling was occasionally used in Britain, the American scientific language used <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> from the start.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinion2005_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinion2005-94">[90]</a></sup> Most scientists used <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> throughout the world in the 19th century;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards18964_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards18964-95">[91]</a></sup> it still remains the standard in most other languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell2015_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Powell2015-91">[87]</a></sup> In 1828, American lexicographer <a href="/wiki/Noah_Webster" title="Noah Webster">Noah Webster</a> used exclusively the <i>aluminum</i> spelling in his <i><a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_Dictionary#First_edition_1828" title="Webster's Dictionary">American Dictionary of the English Language</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup> In the 1830s, the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling started to gain usage in the United States; by the 1860s, it had become the more common spelling there outside science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinion2005_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Quinion2005-94">[90]</a></sup> In 1892, Hall used the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling in his advertising handbill for his new electrolytic method of producing the metal, despite his constant use of the <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> spelling in all the patents he filed between 1886 and 1903. It was subsequently suggested this was a typo rather than intended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Powell2015_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Powell2015-91">[87]</a></sup> By 1890, both spellings had been common in the U.S. overall, the <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> spelling being slightly more common; by 1895, the situation had reversed; by 1900, <i>aluminum</i> had been twice as common as <i>aluminium</i>; during the following decade, the <i><span class="nowrap">-um</span></i> spelling dominated American usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-97">[93]</a></sup> In 1925, the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> adopted this spelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-97">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) adopted <i>aluminium</i> as the standard international name for the element in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-98">[94]</a></sup> In 1993, they recognized <i>aluminum</i> as an acceptable variant;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-98">[94]</a></sup> the same is true for the most recent <a href="/wiki/IUPAC_nomenclature_of_inorganic_chemistry_2005" title="IUPAC nomenclature of inorganic chemistry 2005">2005 edition of the IUPAC nomenclature of inorganic chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup> IUPAC official publications use the <i><span class="nowrap">-ium</span></i> spelling as primary but list both where appropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[e]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/English_Wikipedia" title="English Wikipedia">English Wikipedia</a> follows this standard by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:ALUM" title="Wikipedia:ALUM">adopting</a> the "aluminium" spelling as the sole spelling in chemistry-related articles.
</p>
