<p>Aluminium production is highly energy-consuming, and so the producers tend to locate smelters in places where electric power is both plentiful and inexpensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMP_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMP-103">[98]</a></sup> As of 2012, the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelters</a> of aluminium are located in China, Russia, Bahrain, United Arab Emirates, and South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, China was the top producer of aluminium with a world share of fifty-five percent; the next largest producing countries were Russia, Canada, India, and the United Arab Emirates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGS2018_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-BGS2018-102">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Metal_Stocks_in_Society_report" title="Metal Stocks in Society report">Metal Stocks in Society report</a>, the global <a href="/wiki/Per_capita" title="Per capita">per capita</a> stock of aluminium in use in society (i.e. in cars, buildings, electronics etc.) is 80 kg (180 lb). Much of this is in more-developed countries (350–500 kg (770–1,100 lb) per capita) rather than less-developed countries (35 kg (77 lb) per capita).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bayer_process">Bayer process</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bayer_process" title="Bayer process">Bayer process</a></div>
<p>Bauxite is converted to aluminium oxide by the Bayer process. Bauxite is blended for uniform composition and then is ground. The resulting <a href="/wiki/Slurry" title="Slurry">slurry</a> is mixed with a hot solution of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a>; the mixture is then treated in a digester vessel at a pressure well above atmospheric, dissolving the aluminium hydroxide in bauxite while converting impurities into a relatively insoluble compounds:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-106">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">Al(OH)<sub>3</sub> + Na<sup>+</sup> + OH<sup>−</sup> → Na<sup>+</sup> + [Al(OH)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup></div>
<p>After this reaction, the slurry is at a temperature above its atmospheric boiling point. It is cooled by removing steam as pressure is reduced. The bauxite residue is separated from the solution and discarded. The solution, free of solids, is seeded with small crystals of aluminium hydroxide; this causes decomposition of the [Al(OH)<sub>4</sub>]<sup>−</sup> ions to aluminium hydroxide. After about half of aluminium has precipitated, the mixture is sent to classifiers. Small crystals of aluminium hydroxide are collected to serve as seeding agents; coarse particles are reduced to aluminium oxide; excess solution is removed by evaporation, (if needed) purified, and recycled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-106">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hall.E2.80.93H.C3.A9roult_process"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hall–Héroult_process">Hall–Héroult process</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_smelting" title="Aluminium smelting">Aluminium smelting</a></div>
<p>The conversion of alumina to aluminium metal is achieved by the <a href="/wiki/Hall%E2%80%93H%C3%A9roult_process" title="Hall–Héroult process">Hall–Héroult process</a>. In this energy-intensive process, a solution of alumina in a molten (950 and 980 °C (1,740 and 1,800 °F)) mixture of <a href="/wiki/Cryolite" title="Cryolite">cryolite</a> (Na<sub>3</sub>AlF<sub>6</sub>) with <a href="/wiki/Calcium_fluoride" title="Calcium fluoride">calcium fluoride</a> is <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolyzed</a> to produce metallic aluminium. The liquid aluminium metal sinks to the bottom of the solution and is tapped off, and usually cast into large blocks called <a href="/wiki/Bar_stock" title="Bar stock">aluminium billets</a> for further processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="3027" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg/170px-Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg/255px-Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg/340px-Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tovarna_glinice_in_aluminija_Kidri%C4%8Devo_-_kupi_aluminija_1968.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Extrusion" title="Extrusion">Extrusion</a> billets of aluminium</div></div></div>
<p>Anodes of the electrolysis cell are made of carbon—the most resistant material against fluoride corrosion—and either bake at the process or are prebaked. The former, also called Söderberg anodes, are less power-efficient and fumes released during baking are costly to collect, which is why they are being replaced by prebaked anodes even though they save the power, energy, and labor to prebake the cathodes. Carbon for anodes should be preferably pure so that neither aluminium nor the electrolyte is contaminated with ash. Despite carbon's resistivity against corrosion, it is still consumed at a rate of 0.4–0.5 kg per each kilogram of produced aluminium. Cathodes are made of <a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a>; high purity for them is not required because impurities <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(chemistry)" title="Leaching (chemistry)">leach</a> only very slowly. Cathode is consumed at a rate of 0.02–0.04 kg per each kilogram of produced aluminium. A cell is usually a terminated after 2–6 years following a failure of the cathode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Hall–Heroult process produces aluminium with a purity of above 99%. Further purification can be done by the <a href="/wiki/Hoopes_process" title="Hoopes process">Hoopes process</a>. This process involves the electrolysis of molten aluminium with a sodium, barium, and aluminium fluoride electrolyte. The resulting aluminium has a purity of 99.99%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Electric power represents about 20 to 40% of the cost of producing aluminium, depending on the location of the smelter. Aluminium production consumes roughly 5% of electricity generated in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emsley2011_98-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emsley2011-98">[94]</a></sup> Because of this, alternatives to the Hall–Héroult process have been researched, but none has turned out to be economically feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg/220px-Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg/330px-Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg/440px-Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waste_bins_recyclable.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common bins for recyclable waste along with a bin for unrecyclable waste. The bin with a yellow top is labeled "aluminium". Rhodes, Greece.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_recycling" title="Aluminium recycling">Aluminium recycling</a></div>
<p>Recovery of the metal through <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> has become an important task of the aluminium industry. Recycling was a low-profile activity until the late 1960s, when the growing use of aluminium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverage_can" title="Beverage can">beverage cans</a> brought it to public awareness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup> Recycling involves melting the scrap, a process that requires only 5% of the energy used to produce aluminium from ore, though a significant part (up to 15% of the input material) is lost as <a href="/wiki/Dross" title="Dross">dross</a> (ash-like oxide).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup> An aluminium stack melter produces significantly less dross, with values reported below 1%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>White dross from primary aluminium production and from secondary recycling operations still contains useful quantities of aluminium that can be <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_dross_recycling" title="Aluminium dross recycling">extracted industrially</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[106]</a></sup> The process produces aluminium billets, together with a highly complex waste material. This waste is difficult to manage. It reacts with water, releasing a mixture of gases (including, among others, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>), which spontaneously ignites on contact with air;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[107]</a></sup> contact with damp air results in the release of copious quantities of ammonia gas. Despite these difficulties, the waste is used as a filler in <a href="/wiki/Asphalt" title="Asphalt">asphalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="2074" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg/220px-Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg/330px-Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg/440px-Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Austin_A40_Roadster_ca_1951.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aluminium-bodied <a href="/wiki/Austin_A40_Sports" title="Austin A40 Sports">Austin A40 Sports</a> (c. 1951)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal">Metal</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_alloy" title="Aluminium alloy">Aluminium alloy</a></div>
<p>Aluminium is the most widely used <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">non-ferrous metal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup> The global production of aluminium in 2016 was 58.8 million metric tons. It exceeded that of any other metal except <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (1,231 million metric tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BGS2018_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-BGS2018-102">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is almost always alloyed, which markedly improves its mechanical properties, especially when <a href="/wiki/Tempering_(metallurgy)" title="Tempering (metallurgy)">tempered</a>. For example, the common <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_foil" title="Aluminium foil">aluminium foils</a> and beverage cans are alloys of 92% to 99% aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[110]</a></sup> The main <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alloying" title="Alloying">alloying</a> agents are <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Duralumin" title="Duralumin">duralumin</a>) with the levels of other metals in a few percent by weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg/220px-Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg/330px-Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg/440px-Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Drinking_can_ring-pull_tab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_can" title="Aluminium can">Aluminium can</a></div></div></div>
<p>The major uses for aluminium metal are in:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Transportation (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a>, aircraft, <a href="/wiki/Truck" title="Truck">trucks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railway_car" title="Railway car">railway cars</a>, marine vessels, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle" title="Bicycle">bicycles</a>, spacecraft, etc.). Aluminium is used because of its low density;</li>
<li>Packaging (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_can" title="Aluminium can">cans</a>, foil, frame etc.). Aluminium is used because it is non-toxic, non-<a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">adsorptive</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Splinter" title="Splinter">splinter</a>-proof;</li>
<li>Building and construction (<a href="/wiki/Window" title="Window">windows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Door" title="Door">doors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siding" title="Siding">siding</a>, building wire, sheathing, roofing, etc.). Since steel is cheaper, aluminium is used when lightness, corrosion resistance, or engineering features are important;</li>
<li>Electricity-related uses (conductor alloys, motors and generators, transformers, capacitors, etc.). Aluminium is used because it is relatively cheap, highly conductive, has adequate mechanical strength and low density, and resists corrosion;</li>
<li>A wide range of <a href="/wiki/Household" title="Household">household</a> items, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cooking_utensil" title="Cooking utensil">cooking utensils</a> to <a href="/wiki/Furniture" title="Furniture">furniture</a>. Low density, good appearance, ease of fabrication, and durability are the key factors of aluminium usage;</li>
<li>Machinery and equipment (processing equipment, pipes, tools). Aluminium is used because of its corrosion resistance, non-pyrophoricity, and mechanical strength.</li>
<li>Aluminium <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coins</a> have been produced for small denomination currency, owing to its low manufacturing cost and negligible <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a> value.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h3>
<p>The great majority (about 90%) of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_oxide" title="Aluminium oxide">aluminium oxide</a> is converted to metallic aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-106">[101]</a></sup> Being a very hard material (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohs_hardness" title="Mohs hardness">Mohs hardness</a> 9),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lumley2010_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lumley2010-118">[113]</a></sup> alumina is widely used as an abrasive;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortensen2006_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortensen2006-119">[114]</a></sup> being extraordinarily chemically inert, it is useful in highly reactive environments such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_pressure_sodium" title="High pressure sodium">high pressure sodium</a> lamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan2012-120">[115]</a></sup> Aluminium oxide is commonly used as a catalyst for industrial processes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-106">[101]</a></sup> e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Claus_process" title="Claus process">Claus process</a> to convert <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> to sulfur in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refineries" title="Refineries">refineries</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Alkylation" title="Alkylation">alkylate</a> <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slesser1988_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Slesser1988-121">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Supp2013_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Supp2013-122">[117]</a></sup> Many industrial <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalysts</a> are <a href="/wiki/Catalyst_support" title="Catalyst support">supported</a> by alumina, meaning that the expensive catalyst material is dispersed over a surface of the inert alumina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ErtlKnözinger2008_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-ErtlKnözinger2008-123">[118]</a></sup> Another principal use is as a drying agent or absorbent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannOxide_106-4"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannOxide-106">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArmaregoChai2009_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArmaregoChai2009-124">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3952" data-file-width="2782" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg/220px-Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg/330px-Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg/440px-Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pulsed_Laser_Deposition_in_Action.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Laser deposition of alumina on a substrate</div></div></div>
<p>Several sulfates of aluminium have industrial and commercial application. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">Aluminium sulfate</a> (in its hydrate form) is produced on the annual scale of several millions of metric tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> About two thirds is consumed in <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">water treatment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> The next major application is in the manufacture of paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> It is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Mordant" title="Mordant">mordant</a> in dyeing, in pickling seeds, deodorizing of mineral oils, in <a href="/wiki/Tanning_(leather)" title="Tanning (leather)">leather tanning</a>, and in production of other aluminium compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-3"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> Two kinds of alum, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonium_alum" title="Ammonium alum">ammonium alum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_alum" title="Potassium alum">potassium alum</a>, were formerly used as <a href="/wiki/Mordant" title="Mordant">mordants</a> and in leather tanning, but their use has significantly declined following availability of high-purity aluminium sulfate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-4"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> Anhydrous <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_chloride" title="Aluminium chloride">aluminium chloride</a> is used as a catalyst in chemical and petrochemical industries, the dyeing industry, and in synthesis of various inorganic and organic compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-5"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> Aluminium hydroxychlorides are used in purifying water, in the paper industry, and as antiperspirants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-6"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup> Sodium aluminate is used in treating water and as an accelerator of solidification of cement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannInorganic_125-7"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannInorganic-125">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many aluminium compounds have niche applications, for example:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_acetate" title="Aluminium acetate">Aluminium acetate</a> in solution is used as an <a href="/wiki/Astringent" title="Astringent">astringent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[121]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_phosphate" title="Aluminium phosphate">Aluminium phosphate</a> is used in the manufacture of glass, ceramic, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" title="Wood pulp">pulp</a> and paper products, <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">cosmetics</a>, paints, <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnishes</a>, and in dental <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[122]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">Aluminium hydroxide</a> is used as an <a href="/wiki/Antacid" title="Antacid">antacid</a>, and mordant; it is used also in <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> purification, the manufacture of glass and ceramics, and in the <a href="/wiki/Waterproofing" title="Waterproofing">waterproofing</a> <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">fabrics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-papich_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-papich-129">[124]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lithium_aluminium_hydride" title="Lithium aluminium hydride">Lithium aluminium hydride</a> is a powerful reducing agent used in <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Organoaluminium_chemistry" title="Organoaluminium chemistry">Organoaluminiums</a> are used as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_acid" title="Lewis acid">Lewis acids</a> and cocatalysts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Methylaluminoxane" title="Methylaluminoxane">Methylaluminoxane</a> is a cocatalyst for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ziegler%E2%80%93Natta" title="Ziegler–Natta">Ziegler–Natta</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olefin" title="Olefin">olefin</a> <a href="/wiki/Polymerization" title="Polymerization">polymerization</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Vinyl_polymer" title="Vinyl polymer">vinyl polymers</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyethene" title="Polyethene">polyethene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup></li>
<li>Aqueous aluminium ions (such as aqueous aluminium sulfate) are used to treat against fish parasites such as <i><a href="/wiki/Gyrodactylus_salaris" title="Gyrodactylus salaris">Gyrodactylus salaris</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AasKlemetsen2011_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-AasKlemetsen2011-134">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>In many <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a>, certain aluminium salts serve as an immune <a href="/wiki/Immunologic_adjuvant" title="Immunologic adjuvant">adjuvant</a> (immune response booster) to allow the <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> in the vaccine to achieve sufficient potency as an immune stimulant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh2007_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2007-135">[130]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology">Biology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="647" data-file-width="780" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg/330px-Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg/495px-Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg/660px-Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al_absorption_by_skin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of aluminium absorption by human skin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-health1_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-health1-136">[131]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Despite its widespread occurrence in the Earth crust, aluminium has no known function in biology. Aluminium salts are remarkably nontoxic, <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">aluminium sulfate</a> having an <a href="/wiki/Median_lethal_dose" title="Median lethal dose">LD<sub>50</sub></a> of 6207 mg/kg (oral, mouse), which corresponds to 500 grams for an 80 kg (180 lb) person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ullmann_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span></h3>
<p>In most people, aluminium is not as toxic as <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>. Aluminium is classified as a non-carcinogen by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Health_and_Human_Services" title="United States Department of Health and Human Services">United States Department of Health and Human Services</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> There is little evidence that normal exposure to aluminium presents a risk to healthy adult,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gitelman_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-gitelman-138">[133]</a></sup> and there is evidence of no toxicity if it is consumed in amounts not greater than 40 mg/day per kg of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_weight" title="Body weight">body mass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> Most aluminium consumed will leave the body in feces; the small part of it that enters the body, will be excreted via urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup> Aluminium that does stay in the body is accumulated in, above all, bone; and apart from that, in brain, liver, and kidney.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> Aluminium metal cannot pass the blood–brain barrier and natural filters before the brain, but some compounds, such as the fluoride, can.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balch2006_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balch2006-140">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h3>
<p>Aluminium, although rarely, can cause vitamin D-resistant <a href="/wiki/Osteomalacia" title="Osteomalacia">osteomalacia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">erythropoietin</a>-resistant <a href="/wiki/Microcytic_anemia" title="Microcytic anemia">microcytic anemia</a>, and central nervous system alterations. People with kidney insufficiency are especially at a risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> Chronic ingestion of hydrated aluminium silicates (for excess gastric acidity control) may result in aluminium binding to intestinal contents and increased elimination of other metals, such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>; sufficiently high doses (&gt;50 g/day) can cause anemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-4"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> Since aluminium is excreted by kidneys, their function may be impaired by toxic amounts of aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balch2006_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-Balch2006-140">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="489" data-file-width="762" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg/330px-Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg/495px-Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg/660px-Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Al_transport_across_human_cells.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>There are five major aluminium forms absorbed by human body: the free solvated trivalent cation (Al<sup>3+</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); low-molecular-weight, neutral, soluble complexes (LMW-Al<sup>0</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); high-molecular-weight, neutral, soluble complexes (HMW-Al<sup>0</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); low-molecular-weight, charged, soluble complexes (LMW-Al(L)<sub>n</sub><sup>+/−</sup><sub>(aq)</sub>); nano and micro-particulates (Al(L)<sub>n(s)</sub>). They are transported across cell membranes or cell epi-/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothelia" title="Endothelia">endothelia</a> through five major routes: (1) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracellular" title="Paracellular">paracellular</a>; (2) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transcellular" title="Transcellular">transcellular</a>; (3) <a href="/wiki/Active_transport" title="Active transport">active transport</a>; (4) channels; (5) adsorptive or receptor-mediated <a href="/wiki/Endocytosis" title="Endocytosis">endocytosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-health1_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-health1-136">[131]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>An accident in England revealed that millimolar quantities of aluminium in drinking water cause significant cognitive deficits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-5"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> Orally ingested aluminium salts can deposit in the brain. There is research on correlation between neurological disorders, including <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[f]</a></sup> and aluminium levels, but it has been inconclusive so far.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-6"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium increases <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">estrogen</a>-related <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> in human <a href="/wiki/Breast_cancer" title="Breast cancer">breast cancer</a> cells cultured in the laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup> In very high doses, aluminium is associated with altered function of the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> A small percentage of people<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BinghamCohrssen2012_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-BinghamCohrssen2012-147">[141]</a></sup> have contact <a href="/wiki/Allergy" title="Allergy">allergies</a> to aluminium and experience itchy red rashes, headache, muscle pain, joint pain, poor memory, insomnia, depression, asthma, irritable bowel syndrome, or other symptoms upon contact with products containing aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to powdered aluminium or aluminium welding fumes can cause <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_fibrosis" title="Pulmonary fibrosis">pulmonary fibrosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup> Fine aluminium powder can ignite or explode, posing another workplace hazard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exposure_routes">Exposure routes</span></h3>
<p>Food is the main source of aluminium. Drinking water contains more aluminium than solid food;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-7"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> however, aluminium in food may be absorbed more than aluminium from water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yokel2008_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yokel2008-152">[146]</a></sup> Major sources of human oral exposure to aluminium include food (due to its use in food additives, food and beverage packaging, and cooking utensils), drinking water (due to its use in municipal water treatment), and aluminium-containing medications (particularly antacid/antiulcer and buffered aspirin formulations).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup> Dietary exposure in Europeans averages to 0.2–1.5 mg/kg/week but can be as high as 2.3 mg/kg/week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-8"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> Higher exposure levels of aluminium are mostly limited to miners, aluminium production workers, and <a href="/wiki/Dialysis" title="Dialysis">dialysis</a> patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-154">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Excessive consumption of <a href="/wiki/Antacid" title="Antacid">antacids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiperspirants" title="Antiperspirants">antiperspirants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a>, and cosmetics provide significant exposure levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChenThyssen2018_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChenThyssen2018-155">[149]</a></sup> Consumption of acidic foods or liquids with aluminium enhances aluminium absorption,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[150]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Maltol" title="Maltol">maltol</a> has been shown to increase the accumulation of aluminium in nerve and bone tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[151]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span></h3>
<p>In case of suspected sudden intake of a large amount of aluminium, the only treatment is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deferoxamine_mesylate" title="Deferoxamine mesylate">deferoxamine mesylate</a> which may be given to help eliminate aluminium from the body by <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup> However, this should be applied with caution as this reduces not only aluminium body levels, but also those of other metals such as copper or iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">[152]</a></sup> Nutritionally, treatment of similar to those of other toxic metals and includes removal of sources of aluminium from environment, enhancing cellular energy production, enhancing activity of the eliminative organs, and chelating aluminium with nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-158">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2287" data-file-width="3443" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg/220px-Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg/330px-Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg/440px-Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luftaufnahmen_Nordseekueste_2012-05-by-RaBoe-478.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Bauxite_tailings" title="Bauxite tailings">Bauxite tailings</a>" storage facility in <a href="/wiki/Stade" title="Stade">Stade</a>, Germany. The aluminium industry generates about 70 million tons of this waste annually.</div></div></div><p>High levels of aluminium occur near mining cites; small amounts of aluminium are released to the environment at the coal-fired power plants or <a href="/wiki/Incineration" title="Incineration">incinerators</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-160">[154]</a></sup> Aluminium in the air is washed out by the rain or normally settles down but small particles of aluminium remain in the air for a long time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-160">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Acidic <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> is the main natural factor to mobilize aluminium from natural sources<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-9"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup> and the main reason for the environmental effects of aluminium;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosselandEldhuset1990_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosselandEldhuset1990-161">[155]</a></sup> however, the main factor of presence of aluminium in salt and freshwater are the industrial processes that also release aluminium into air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piero3_137-10"><a href="#cite_note-Piero3-137">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In water, aluminium acts as a toxiс agent on <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a>-breathing animals such as <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> by causing loss of <a href="/wiki/Blood_plasma" title="Blood plasma">plasma</a>- and <a href="/wiki/Hemolymph" title="Hemolymph">hemolymph</a> ions leading to <a href="/wiki/Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation">osmoregulatory</a> failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosselandEldhuset1990_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-RosselandEldhuset1990-161">[155]</a></sup> Organic complexes of aluminium may be easily absorbed and interfere with metabolism in mammals and birds, even though this rarely happens in practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosselandEldhuset1990_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-RosselandEldhuset1990-161">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium is primary among the factors that reduce plant growth on acidic soils. Although it is generally harmless to plant growth in pH-neutral soils, in acid soils the concentration of toxic Al<sup>3+</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cation" title="Cation">cations</a> increases and disturbs root growth and function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[159]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">Wheat</a> has <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">developed</a> a tolerance to aluminium, releasing <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> that bind to harmful aluminium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cations" title="Cations">cations</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">Sorghum</a> is believed to have the same tolerance mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aluminium production possesses its own challenges to the environment on each step of the production process. The major challenge is the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-154">[148]</a></sup> These gases result from electrical consumption of the smelters and the byproducts of processing. The most potent of these gases are <a href="/wiki/Fluorocarbon" title="Fluorocarbon">perfluorocarbons</a> from the smelting process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-:4-154">[148]</a></sup> Released <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> is one of the primary precursors of <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_154-3"><a href="#cite_note-:4-154">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Spanish scientific report from 2001 claimed that the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Geotrichum_candidum" title="Geotrichum candidum">Geotrichum candidum</a></i> consumes the aluminium in <a href="/wiki/Compact_disc" title="Compact disc">compact discs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup> Other reports all refer back to that report and there is no supporting original research. Better documented, the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_aeruginosa" title="Pseudomonas aeruginosa">Pseudomonas aeruginosa</a></i> and the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Cladosporium" title="Cladosporium">Cladosporium</a> resinae</i> are commonly detected in aircraft fuel tanks that use <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>-based fuels (not <a href="/wiki/Avgas" title="Avgas">avgas</a>), and laboratory cultures can degrade aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[163]</a></sup> However, these life forms do not directly attack or consume the aluminium; rather, the metal is corroded by microbe waste products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[164]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">No elements with odd atomic numbers have more than two stable isotopes; even-numbered elements have multiple stable isotopes, with tin (element 50) having the highest number of isotopes of all elements, ten.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEA_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEA-9">[9]</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Even_and_odd_atomic_nuclei" title="Even and odd atomic nuclei">Even and odd atomic nuclei</a> for more details.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most other metals have greater standard atomic weights: for instance, that of iron is 55.8; copper 63.5; lead 207.2.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAAW2016_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-CIAAW2016-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aluminium's low density (compared to the other metals) arises from the fact that its nuclei are much lighter, while difference in the unit cell size does not compensate for this difference.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Abundances in the source are listed relative to silicon rather than in per-particle notation. The sum of all elements per 10<sup>6</sup> parts of silicon is 2.6682<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">10</span></sup> parts; aluminium comprises 8.410<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">4</span></sup> parts.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For instance, see the November–December 2013 issue of <i>Chemistry International</i>: in a table of (some) elements, the element is listed as "aluminium (aluminum)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_Society" title="Alzheimer's Society">Alzheimer's Society</a>, the medical and scientific opinion is that studies have not convincingly demonstrated a causal relationship between aluminium and Alzheimer's disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alzheimersSociety_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-alzheimersSociety-141">[136]</a></sup> Research in this area has been inconclusive; aluminium accumulation may be a consequence of the disease rather than a causal agent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[138]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
